Extraneous load. Unnecessary load distracts from learning the pattern. Obvious distractions that eat up working memory, such as television in the background, make learning harder. But extraneous load also includes mental work needed to learn a subject that isnât necessary. Poorly organized study materials can increase cognitive load. Examples of this include having to flip between pages to understand a diagram, or making students figure out a pattern that could be taught explicitly.
Germane load. Efforts that improve learning outcomes but are not strictly necessary to learn the pattern. Some forms of germane load include self-explanations and retrieval practice, both of which are effortful but increase the ability to recall a pattern later.34
Initially, I found germane load confusing. If excessive cognitive load impedes learning, isnât the category of âgermaneâ load just a sneaky way of saying sometimes it doesnât?

Not quite. Working memory has a fixed capacity. If the intrinsic load fills the entire available space, any additional load will be harmful. However, if intrinsic load is not near the maximum, the âspareâ capacity can be used for activities that deepen learning.


Consider variable practice, the idea of practicing a skill with an increased range of problems and in different contexts. Itâs harder than practice which occurs in only a narrow range of problems. Yet, thereâs evidence that variable practice leads to better learning and transfer.5

However, the learning benefit of variable practice only occurs when cognitive load isnât overwhelmed. If it is, then simpler forms of practice become preferable.6

Key Experiments in Cognitive Load Theory
Over the past few decades, cognitive load theory has amassed a lot of interesting experimental effects with catchy-sounding names. Here are a few:

1. The Worked-Example Effect
Traditionally, math education has focused on having students solve problems to get good at math. Sweller and Cooper pushed back against this idea, showing that studying worked examples (problems, along with detailed solutions) is often more efficient.7


Worked examples have since been shown to be powerful tools in many domains. The rationale is that problem solving is a cognitively demanding activity. This creates a lot of extraneous load, making it harder to abstract what the general solution procedure involves.

Sweller and Cooper, of course, agree that practice is helpful. But they argue in favor of presenting lots of examples first. In their model, practice should start with access to examples so students can emulate the pattern. Finally, practice without the solutions available becomes helpful when the material is learned well enough that retrieval efforts are germane load rather than just too much.

2. The Goal-Free Effect
One reason problem solving is difficult is that it requires you to keep in mind the goal youâre trying to reach, how far you are from the goal, and potential operations to move forward. This creates a lot of cognitive load that makes it harder to identify the solution procedure.

Removing an explicit goal can also reduce cognitive load. For example, a classic trigonometry problem might ask a student to find a particular angle. A âgoal-freeâ way to present this would be to ask students to find as many angles as possible.


Research shows that early, goal-free problems result in greater learning, consistent with cognitive load theory.8

The downside of goal-free practice, however, is that if there are too many possible actions, most of those explored will be useless. Solving a trigonometry puzzle with several unknowns is helpful. But learning to program by randomly typing in commands is not. Worked examples tend to be a more general tool, since they enable useful patterns to be learned rather than guessed at.

3. The Split-Attention Effect
Cognitive load isnât just found in problem solving. Badly designed instructional materials can increase cognitive load by requiring learners to move their attention around to understand them.9

Consider these two flashcards for learning Chinese characters. The first creates extra cognitive load since the pairing between sound and character requires more spatial manipulation. Learning is enhanced when instructional materials are organized so that information doesnât require any manipulation to be understood.


4. The Expertise-Reversal Effect
Cognitive load theory predicts that for novices exposed to information for the first time, worked examples are better than problem solving. But, interestingly, this effect reverses as you gain more experience
One explanation for this is in terms of redundancy. If the solution pattern is already stored in your long-term memory, making sense of a worked example doesnât help much. In this case, it is better to retrieve the answer directly from memory without distracting yourself with the example.

Another explanation is that if the problems are reasonably easy to solve, worked examples may not provoke deep enough processing. Solving a problem yourself is a kind of germane load akin to retrieval practice.ChatGPTCognitive load theory, as described in the text, provides valuable insights into the challenges of learning and offers strategies to make learning more effective. Here are some key takeaways from the text:Limited Mental Bandwidth: The theory suggests that our working memory has limitations when dealing with new information, but it has no such limitations when dealing with previously mastered material. This limitation can make learning new, complex subjects challenging.Effortless Learning: Some skills and subjects are learned effortlessly because humans are evolutionarily predisposed to learn certain patterns of information. These innate skills and subjects are acquired without conscious, effortful cognitive processing.Three Types of Cognitive Load: Cognitive load theory distinguishes three types of cognitive load:Intrinsic Load: The cognitive load necessary to learn a new pattern and store it in long-term memory.Extraneous Load: Unnecessary load that distracts from learning, including distractions in the environment or poorly organized study materials.Germane Load: Efforts that enhance learning outcomes but are not strictly necessary for learning the pattern. Examples include self-explanations and retrieval practice.Optimizing Learning: The theory highlights the importance of optimizing the use of working memory. When intrinsic load is not near its maximum, there is "spare" capacity that can be used for activities like variable practice, which enhances learning. However, if cognitive load is overwhelming, simpler forms of practice become preferable.Key Experiments: Cognitive load theory has been supported by various experiments, including:The Worked-Example Effect: Studying worked examples (problems with detailed solutions) can be more efficient than traditional problem-solving approaches in learning.The Goal-Free Effect: Removing explicit goals from learning tasks can reduce cognitive load and improve learning outcomes.The Split-Attention Effect: Poorly designed instructional materials that require learners to move their attention around can increase cognitive load and hinder learning.The Expertise-Reversal Effect: The effectiveness of worked examples versus problem-solving may reverse as learners gain more experience. For novices, worked examples are often more effective, but as expertise grows, problem-solving may become more beneficial.These insights from cognitive load theory have practical applications in education and instructional design. By understanding and managing the cognitive load placed on learners, educators can create more effective teaching materials and strategies, ultimately enhancing the learning experience.Cognitive Load Theory and its Applications for Learning

2. The Goal-Free Effect
One reason problem solving is difficult is that it requires you to keep in mind the goal youâre trying to reach, how far you are from the goal, and potential operations to move forward. This creates a lot of cognitive load that makes it harder to identify the solution procedure.

Removing an explicit goal can also reduce cognitive load. For example, a classic trigonometry problem might ask a student to find a particular angle. A âgoal-freeâ way to present this would be to ask students to find as many angles as possible.


Research shows that early, goal-free problems result in greater learning, consistent with cognitive load theory.8

The downside of goal-free practice, however, is that if there are too many possible actions, most of those explored will be useless. Solving a trigonometry puzzle with several unknowns is helpful. But learning to program by randomly typing in commands is not. Worked examples tend to be a more general tool, since they enable useful patterns to be learned rather than guessed at.

3. The Split-Attention Effect
Cognitive load isnât just found in problem solving. Badly designed instructional materials can increase cognitive load by requiring learners to move their attention around to understand them.9

Consider these two flashcards for learning Chinese characters. The first creates extra cognitive load since the pairing between sound and character requires more spatial manipulation. Learning is enhanced when instructional materials are organized so that information doesnât require any manipulation to be understood.


4. The Expertise-Reversal Effect
Cognitive load theory predicts that for novices exposed to information for the first time, worked examples are better than problem solving. But, interestingly, this effect reverses as you gain more experience.10


One explanation for this is in terms of redundancy. If the solution pattern is already stored in your long-term memory, making sense of a worked example doesnât help much. In this case, it is better to retrieve the answer directly from memory without distracting yourself with the example.

Another explanation is that if the problems are reasonably easy to solve, worked examples may not provoke deep enough processing. Solving a problem yourself is a kind of germane load akin to retrieval practice.

Applying Cognitive Load Theory to Your Studies
Cognitive load theoryâs principal applications are in instructional design. How should a subject be taught so that students will efficiently master the patterns of knowledge it contains? Cognitive load theory favors direct instruction, quick feedback and plenty of practice.

However, as learners, weâre often just given instructional materials. What can we do to optimize cognitive load, given that perfect explanations and studying resources arenât always available?

Here are a few suggestions:

1. Study examples before solving problems.
While some amount of âfiguring things outâ is often the only path available, this can make it harder to grasp the key concepts. There are a few tools you can apply, as a learner, to make this easier:

Look for examples online. Khan Academy and many other websites offer detailed instructions and worked examples for common problems.
Look for problem sets with solutions. This was a big part of my MIT Challenge. Copious problem sets with solutions let you shift between studying the steps of a worked solution and practicing it yourself. This approach tends to beat instructions that only talk about problem solving at a general level (and omit the specifics of a worked example). It also allows you to shift to solving problems yourself once youâve gotten a good grasp of the material.
Self-explain your homework when given feedback. In a traditional class, solutions often arenât provided until long after the homework assignment. In this case, after you get the solutions, spend the time to thoroughly explain to yourself the solution to problems you found difficult. Self-explanations are a germane load that ensures your homework feedback is put to good use.
This approach applies to non-technical subjects as well. When learning to paint, I made heavy use of video tutorials where I worked on the same painting as the instructor. Iâd usually watch the video through once, then work alongside the instructor on a second pass.

2. If a class confuses you, slow it down early.
In my experience, the Feynman Technique mainly works by slowing things down. A concept can be confusing in a lecture because critical assumptions arenât made explicit or intervening steps are skipped. Walking through the explanation yourself lets you figure out exactly where you get lost.

A difficult class is one where cognitive load is near your maximum. Sometimes it will go too far, and youâll get lost. Catching these moments early and fixing them is a big part of staying on top of your studies. Since omitted knowledge is often reused in later parts of the class, failing to understand something important in an early lecture can mean the rest of the class time is wasted.

3. Build your prerequisite knowledge and procedural fluency.
Cognitive load theory is most important in domains where there is great element interactivity. This means that many different pieces of information all need to be in place before you can understand the problem. In contrast, a subject might have extensive difficulty. In this case, there may be a large body of information to learn, but you rarely need all of it at once.

Math and science tend to have high element interactivity, which is why mastery of them is seen as a sign of intelligence. Working memory is associated with intelligence, and those with slightly more working memory can handle slightly greater element interactivity. While this creates only a modest advantage in the short term, greater ease in learning basic concepts can accumulate into a considerable advantage in the long run.

If youâre struggling in a subject with high element interactivity, the key is to go back and invest in more practice in the underlying skills. Doing this will make you more fluent in the component knowledge, which frees up more working memory for handling the new topics.

My Changing Views on Cognitive Load
Iâll confess, I didnât fully appreciate cognitive load theory when I first encountered it. I tended to equate âproblem solvingâ with âpractice.â Since practice is essential for learning, I reasoned that problem solving must be equally important. Real life involves a lot of problem solving, so why shouldnât you practice it?

There seem to be two good answers to my misconception:

Problem solving isnât a skill. The way we get good at solving problems is by having (a) knowledge that assists in solving the problem and (b) automatic procedural components that help in solving problems. There are probably no general problem solving methods that work for every domain. Heuristics for problem solving within a domain might exist. Still, the significance of these is overwhelmed by the power of having tons of learned patterns in memory. This explains why transfer is elusive and why expertise tends to be specific.
Practice improves fluency, but itâs most efficient to have the right method first. It is critical for complex skills with many interacting parts. Figuring out what works through trial and error is inefficient. Worked examples, clear instructions, and background knowledge all help to put practice on the right tracks.
When I discussed these revelations with a friend, he asked how they might have changed my previous learning projects. I can think of a few places where I made mistakes:

During my portrait drawing challenge, I initially focused on getting lots of practice with feedback. However, taking the class with Vitruvian Studios made the most significant difference. A good method can save countless hours of practice.
Cognitive load theory helps me make sense of the optimal time to start immersion when learning a language. For Vat and I, the ~50 hours we spent on Spanish was enough to get going relatively smoothly. Yet even 100 hours in Chinese was still a bit of a grind for me when we first arrived. For Korean, we ended up doing most of the preparatory work in Seoul, which was a somewhat wasted opportunity. Cognitive load theory helps explain how the design choices Vat and I made on the trip made some parts more successful than others. (For instance, Google Translate was a great way to alleviate cognitive load in speaking situations that otherwise would have been above our level.)
The cognitive load was too high in my quantum mechanics project. Part of this was the several-year gap I had since using calculus and differential equations. The components werenât as fresh, so I was relearning a little too much. But a bigger part was that I didnât have as many problem sets with solutions as I would have liked. If I had more, I could have used the first batch as worked examples rather than needing to use them sparingly. In the future, Iâd probably do some warm-up to refresh my prerequisite skills and seek out a textbook with tons of sample problems and solutions so I could study with a tighter feedback loop.
Even after fifteen years of obsessing about the topic, Iâm always working to refine my learning process. As always, Iâll continue to share what I find with you.

It emphasizes the importance of managing cognitive load to facilitate more effective learning and problem-solving strategies.More about the zone of proximal development.ChatGPTThe Zone of Proximal Development (ZPD) is a concept introduced by Lev Vygotsky, a renowned Russian psychologist, in the field of developmental psychology and educational theory. It refers to the range of tasks or activities that a learner can perform with the help or guidance of a more knowledgeable person, such as a teacher, mentor, or peer, but cannot yet do independently.Here are some key points to understand about the Zone of Proximal Development:Dynamic Nature: The ZPD is not a fixed or static concept. It evolves as the learner gains new knowledge and skills. Tasks that were initially within the ZPD may move out of it as the learner becomes more proficient.Scaffolding: Vygotsky emphasized the role of a "scaffold" provided by a more knowledgeable person to support the learner within their ZPD. This scaffolding can include guidance, hints, explanations, or modeling to help the learner complete tasks they couldn't manage alone.Cultural and Social Context: Vygotsky's theory highlights the importance of cultural and social context in learning. He believed that learning is deeply connected to the culture and society in which an individual grows up. As a result, social interactions and cultural norms play a significant role in the ZPD.Developmental Potential: The ZPD represents the learner's potential for development. It identifies tasks that are within reach with appropriate support. Vygotsky argued that focusing on tasks within the ZPD is more effective for promoting cognitive development than simply instructing at the learner's current skill level.Peer Interaction: While Vygotsky's original work focused on the role of adults as facilitators, more recent interpretations of the ZPD also emphasize the importance of peer interactions. Peer learning can provide valuable scaffolding within the ZPD, as learners often explain concepts to each other in ways that are easier to understand.Assessment and Teaching: The ZPD has implications for both assessment and teaching. Educators can use the concept to identify a learner's current level of development and determine appropriate instructional strategies. Instead of teaching too far ahead or too far below a learner's current abilities, educators can aim to stay within the ZPD for optimal learning.Cultural-Historical Theory: Vygotsky's work on the ZPD is closely tied to his cultural-historical theory of development, which suggests that cognitive development is influenced by cultural tools, including language, and that social interaction is a key driver of development.In practical terms, the ZPD encourages educators to provide guided instruction that aligns with a learner's current abilities and pushes them to acquire new knowledge and skills. It emphasizes the importance of responsive teaching and recognizing that learners can accomplish more with support than they can independently.Overall, the Zone of Proximal Development is a crucial concept in educational psychology, highlighting the role of social interaction and scaffolding in promoting cognitive development and effective learning.Give a series of prompts that asks for connections with a topic to zpd theoryChatGPTCertainly! Here are some connections between Zone of Proximal Development (ZPD) theory and Cognitive Load Theory:Complementary Frameworks:ZPD theory and Cognitive Load Theory are complementary frameworks used in education. While ZPD theory focuses on the learner's current level of development and potential for growth, Cognitive Load Theory addresses the cognitive demands placed on learners.Optimizing Learning:Both theories aim to optimize learning outcomes. ZPD theory seeks to provide tasks and guidance that are just challenging enough to promote growth, while Cognitive Load Theory focuses on managing the mental effort required for effective learning.Instructional Design:Educators often use elements of both theories in instructional design. ZPD principles help in tailoring instruction to individual learners, while Cognitive Load Theory guides the design of materials and tasks to minimize cognitive overload.Supporting Novices and Experts:ZPD theory is particularly relevant when teaching novices, helping them bridge the gap between their current knowledge and mastery. Cognitive Load Theory, on the other hand, is valuable for understanding how experts process information efficiently.Resource Allocation:ZPD theory considers the allocation of cognitive resources based on the learner's readiness. Cognitive Load Theory extends this concept by offering strategies for managing those resources effectively during learning.Feedback and Guidance:ZPD theory emphasizes the importance of scaffolding and providing appropriate guidance. Cognitive Load Theory underscores the need to offer clear instructions and minimize extraneous cognitive load during learning.Individual Differences:Both theories acknowledge that learners have varying levels of prior knowledge and cognitive capacity. ZPD theory encourages adapting instruction to these differences, while Cognitive Load Theory provides insights into how to present information most effectively.Application in Technology-Enhanced Learning:In technology-enhanced learning environments, the principles of both ZPD theory and Cognitive Load Theory are often used to create adaptive, learner-centered experiences that optimize learning while managing cognitive resources.By considering the connections between ZPD theory and Cognitive Load Theory, educators and instructional designers can develop more effective teaching strategies and learning materials that align with the learners' developmental levels and cognitive capacities.Here are some connections between the topics we discussed about mathematics and the Zone of Proximal Development (ZPD) theory:

2. **Scaffolding in Mathematics**:
   - ZPD theory emphasizes scaffolding, which is the process of providing structured support to learners. In mathematics, this could involve breaking down complex problems into smaller, more manageable steps, offering hints or prompts, and gradually reducing assistance as the learner progresses.

3. **Differentiated Instruction**:
   - ZPD theory encourages differentiated instruction, tailoring teaching methods and content to individual learners. In mathematics, this means adapting the curriculum to match the diverse mathematical backgrounds and readiness levels of students.

4. **Mathematics Achievement Gap**:
   - ZPD theory can help address the mathematics achievement gap by providing targeted interventions and support to students who may be struggling. Educators can identify where students are in their mathematical development and provide appropriate resources to bridge the gap.

5. **Mathematics Problem Solving**:
   - ZPD theory can guide the teaching of problem-solving skills in mathematics. It suggests presenting students with problems that are slightly above their current abilities, encouraging them to stretch their problem-solving skills while providing necessary support.

6. **Mathematics Technology Tools**:
   - Technology-enhanced learning tools in mathematics can be designed with ZPD principles in mind. These tools can adapt to individual learners, offering challenges and content that align with their ZPD to optimize learning.

   - ZPD theory recognizes the importance of learner confidence. In mathematics, providing tasks that are challenging yet achievable can boost students' confidence in their mathematical abilities.
